  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "# Social distancing"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "Quarantine seems to be very effective, especially when carried out strongly enough and when combined with effective detection of exposed individuals. But you need to get a lot right for it to work. Maybe there's another way?\n",
    "\n",
    "```{margin}\n",
    "\n",
    "Some people *never* develop symptoms: for some reason their immune systems suppress the disease enough to keep them well, but not enough to eliminate it and stop them being infectious. The most famous case of this phenomenon is \"Typhoid Mary\", who infected a large number of people with typhoid fever despite showing no symptoms herself {cite}`MTK13`.\n",
    "```\n",
    "\n",
    "Let's go back to basics. In SEIR-style diseases we have the possibility of individuals walking around and spreading the disease without showing any symptoms. There is by definition no way (absent testing) to spot these individuals until (and unless) they show symptoms. So the challenge is to stop these infectious-but-asymptomatic individuals from coming into contact with susceptible individuals.\n",
    "\n",
    "What is we re-structure the contact network so that people only have contact with a small group of people, and reduce the amount of mixing between those groups? If someone is infected in spite of everything then they'll probably infect their own group, but they'll be less opportunity to infect other groups. This strategy is referred to as **social distancing**, reducing and re-structuring the connections within a population.   "
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "## A socially distanced social network"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "Social distancing is another topological approach to epidemic control. It works by changing the network over which the disease operates, rather than addressing the disease itself. s such it's well-suited to new diseases and those for which there are few effective therapies. "
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 1,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "import numpy\n",
    "import networkx\n",
    "import epyc\n",
    "import epydemic\n",
    "import pandas\n",
    "import mpmath\n",
    "\n",
    "import matplotlib\n",
    "%matplotlib inline\n",
    "%config InlineBackend.figure_formats = {'svg', 'png'}\n",
    "import matplotlib.pyplot as plt\n",
    "import seaborn\n",
    "matplotlib.style.use('seaborn')"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "What does \"social distancing\" mean in network terms? We can understand it best by thinking about the actual conditions of socially-distant lockdown, and then re-creating this structure as a network.\n",
    "\n",
    "The idea of social distancing is to place individuals in small \"bubbles\" of contact &ndash; typically just their own immediate family &ndash; with whom they interact strongly, and weaken the connections that any family member has with individuals in other bubbles. Within a bubble, infection of one person will probably transfer quickly to most or all all of the other members. But the ability of the disease to pass between bubbles is substantially reduced, since there is relatively little contact between them. One can reduce the inter-bubble transmission even further using quarantine when someone realises they are infected.\n",
    "\n",
    "In network terms, the bubbles are {ref}`complete graphs <sec:network-complete>` (everyone in a bubble meets everyone else) whose size depends on the size of the family. We could set all families to the same size, say 4; alternatively we could draw family sizes from some probability distribution where the average size of a family is 4.5 (2 adults with the proverbial 2.5 children) but we allow larger and smaller families to occur. Larger families risk more infections if a member is infected.\n",
    "\n",
    "We now need to link the bubbles. In each bubble, some person or people meet with the outside world: perhaps they're the designated shopper, or someone in a key role whose work brings them into contact with others. We then need to connect these people to other bubbles.\n",
    "\n",
    "This description still leaves quite a lot to be decided:\n",
    "\n",
    "- What is the distribution of family sizes? Are large families really unusual? Or do they follow a \"normal\" distribution?\n",
    "- How many people in each family connect to the outside? Is it always one or two? Might a large family have more contacts?\n",
    "- How do those in contact outside their family group connect with others? Are some people significantly more connected than others?\n",
    "\n",
    "These decisions are simply additional {ref}`degrees of freedom <sec:seir-degrees-of-freedom>` (again) for our model &ndash; with all that this entails.\n",
    "\n",
    "Creating such a network means addressing all these issues. There's a trick we can perform, though. Notice that while the detailed choices change, the structure of the network doesn't. This means that, just as our disease models have parameters that we can change, so does our socially-distanced network &ndash; with the difference that the network parameters are given by choices of probability distributions as well as single numbers. But the solution is the same: define what it means to build the network, providing these distributions as parameters that are \"plugged in\" to the same structure-building process.\n",
    "\n",
    "Building the network involves a complicated piece of code &ndash; the most complicated we've seen so far."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 136,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def social_distance_graph(N, clusterSizeDistribution, contactDistribution, clusterContactDistribution):\n",
    "    # build the initial graph\n",
    "    g = networkx.Graph()\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # build the clusters, each being a complete graph K_s of size s,\n",
    "    # labelled uniquely within the overall graph\n",
    "    rng = numpy.random.default_rng()\n",
    "    n = 0\n",
    "    cid = 1\n",
    "    clusters = []\n",
    "    while n < N:\n",
    "        # build the cluster's graph with a random size\n",
    "        s = clusterSizeDistribution()\n",
    "        K_s = networkx.complete_graph(s)\n",
    "        \n",
    "        # relabel the cluster graph so all nodes in the final\n",
    "        # social distence graph have unique integer labels\n",
    "        networkx.relabel_nodes(K_s, lambda l: n + l, copy=False)\n",
    "        \n",
    "        # add to the graph and list of clusters\n",
    "        # label edges with the cluster they belong to (numbered from 1)\n",
    "        # and the size of the cluster\n",
    "        g.add_nodes_from(K_s.nodes, cluster=cid, cluster_size=s)\n",
    "        g.add_edges_from(K_s.edges, cluster=cid, cluster_size=s)\n",
    "        clusters.append(K_s)\n",
    "        n += s\n",
    "        cid += 1\n",
    "        \n",
    "    # draw the number of contact nodes per cluster from\n",
    "    # from the distribution\n",
    "    contacts = []\n",
    "    for c in clusters:\n",
    "        s = c.order()\n",
    "        d = contactDistribution(s)\n",
    "        contacts.append(d)\n",
    "        \n",
    "    # decide on the arity of each contact node\n",
    "    stubs = []\n",
    "    for i in range(len(clusters)):\n",
    "        c = clusters[i]\n",
    "        ls = list(c.nodes())[:contacts[i]]   # first i node labels (since all nodes are identical in K_s) \n",
    "        for l in ls:\n",
    "            # draw the number of contacts for this node\n",
    "            e = clusterContactDistribution(n)\n",
    "            \n",
    "            # append e copies of the node label to the list of stubs\n",
    "            stubs.extend([l] * e)\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # if number of stubs isn't even, add one to a randomly-chosen node\n",
    "    if len(stubs) % 2 > 0:\n",
    "        us = list(set(stubs))                # don't favour already-high-degree nodes\n",
    "        j = rng.integers(len(us))\n",
    "        stubs.append(us[j])\n",
    "        \n",
    "    # shuffle the stubs until there are no edge pair with the same endpoints\n",
    "    rng.shuffle(stubs)                       # initial shuffle (may leave self-loops)\n",
    "    while True:\n",
    "        # look for pairtings of stubs and others within the same cluster,\n",
    "        # and break them randomly (without changing the degree distribution)\n",
    "        swaps = 0\n",
    "        for i in range(0, len(stubs), 2):\n",
    "            if g.nodes[stubs[i]]['cluster'] == g.nodes[stubs[i + 1]]['cluster']:\n",
    "                # self loop, swap with another randomly-chosen stub\n",
    "                j = rng.integers(len(stubs))\n",
    "                t = stubs[i + 1]\n",
    "                stubs[i + 1] = stubs[j]\n",
    "                stubs[j] = t\n",
    "                \n",
    "                swaps += 1\n",
    "        if swaps == 0:\n",
    "            # no swaps, we're finished\n",
    "            break\n",
    "\n",
    "    # connect the nodes by pulling pairs of stubs and\n",
    "    # creating an edge between them\n",
    "    for i in range(0, len(stubs), 2):\n",
    "        # label inter-bubble edges as cluster 0 of size 0\n",
    "        g.add_edge(stubs[i], stubs[i + 1], cluster=0, cluster_size=0)\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # return the graph and list of cluster sizes\n",
    "    return (g, list(map(lambda h: h.order(), clusters)))"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "This function takes four parameters &ndash; the size of network and three probability-distribution functions &ndash; and returns a network and a list of the bubble sizes. Within the network it creates the social bubbles and labels them uniquely, and then connects the bubbles together randomly. "
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "## Making some choices"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "We can't get away from making choices about the degrees of freedom indefinitely, though &ndash; and in fact that time has arrived.\n",
    "\n",
    "Since we're interested in large-scale phenomena, let's make some simple choices:\n",
    "\n",
    "- Families whose sizes are normally-distributed integers with a mean of 4.5 and a standard deviation of 2\n",
    "- A normal distribution of contacts in each family\n",
    "- Exponentially-distributed links betyween connections, to allow for very connected individuals\n",
    "\n",
    "Since it makes no sense to allow entirely isolated families, those with no size, and \"connected\" individuals with no contacts, we cut off all the distributions with a minimum of 1."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 148,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def averageFamily():\n",
    "    rng = numpy.random.default_rng()\n",
    "    return max(int(rng.normal(4.5, 2)), 1)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 149,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def coupleOfContacts(s):\n",
    "    rng = numpy.random.default_rng()\n",
    "    return max(int(rng.normal(min(s / 2, 2))), 1)    "
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 170,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def expInterBubble(n):\n",
    "    rng = numpy.random.default_rng()\n",
    "    return max(int(rng.exponential(10.0)), 1)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "These three functions, coupled with the network size, are enough to build our network."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 189,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "N = 1000"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 190,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "(g, clusters) = social_distance_graph(N, averageFamily, coupleOfContacts, expInterBubble)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "We can check the various elements of this network. For example, we can check what range of family \"bubble\" sizes we have, and how connected the various contact individuals are."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 191,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [
    {
     "name": "stdout",
     "output_type": "stream",
     "text": [
      "Mean family size of 4.12 (range 1-9)\n",
      "Most connected individual has 69 contacts\n"
     ]
    }
   ],
   "source": [
    "print('Mean family size of {s:.2f} (range {minf}-{maxf})'.format(s=numpy.mean(clusters), minf=min(clusters), maxf=max(clusters)))\n",
    "print('Most connected individual has {k} contacts'.format(k=max(dict(g.degree()).values())))"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "We could also draw the network to inspect it, colouring the nodes with the size of cluster they belong to &ndash; although that turns out not to be especially revealing."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 145,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def draw_social_distance(g, cmap=None, color='cluster_size', ax=None):\n",
    "    # fill in defaults\n",
    "    if cmap is None:\n",
    "        cmap = plt.get_cmap('viridis')\n",
    "    if ax is None:\n",
    "        ax = plt.gca()\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # work out the colours \n",
    "    ncs = list(map(lambda n: g.nodes[n][color], g.nodes()))\n",
    "    ecs = list(map(lambda e: g.edges[e][color], g.edges()))\n",
    "               \n",
    "    # draw with spring layout, which seems to give good results\n",
    "    networkx.draw_spring(g,\n",
    "                         ax=ax,\n",
    "                         with_labels=False,\n",
    "                         node_size=50,\n",
    "                         node_color=ncs, edge_color=ecs,\n",
    "                         cmap=cmap, edge_cmap=cmap)"
   ]

We can see these less-connected clusters, and in particular see that they have a range of colours, indicating that they're of different sizes &ndash; and therefore giving confidence that the algorithm hasn't (for example) only made large clusters highly connected.
